style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Short visual history of furniture styles (from left to right): cloisonné plaque (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_furniture#Assyria" title="Ancient furniture">Assyrian</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chair_of_Reniseneb" title="Chair of Reniseneb">Chair of Reniseneb</a> (Ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Art_of_Ancient_Egypt#Furniture" title="Art of Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a>), metal <a href="/wiki/Brazier" title="Brazier">brazier</a> with <a href="/wiki/Satyr" title="Satyr">satyrs</a> from <a href="/wiki/Pompei" title="Pompei">Pompei</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_furniture#Greece" title="Ancient furniture">Greco</a>-<a href="/wiki/Ancient_furniture#Rome" title="Ancient furniture">Roman</a>), fall-front cabinet inlaid with ivory (<a href="/wiki/Indian_art" title="Indian art">Indian</a>), low-back armchair (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_art#Decorative_arts" title="Chinese art">Chinese</a>), casket with images of <a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">Cupids</a> (<a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine</a>), wood and ivory furniture fragment (<a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic</a>), chest (<a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic</a>), <a href="/wiki/Analogion" title="Analogion">analogion</a> (<a href="/wiki/Romania_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Romania in the Middle Ages">Romanian Medieval</a>), sideboard with two bodies (<a href="/wiki/Renaissance_art" title="Renaissance art">Renaissance</a>), <a href="/wiki/Gilding" title="Gilding">gilded</a> table (<a href="/wiki/Baroque#Furniture" title="Baroque">Baroque</a>), commode (<a href="/wiki/Rococo#Furniture_and_decoration" title="Rococo">Rococo</a>), armchair with <a href="/wiki/Cornucopia" title="Cornucopia">cornucopia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI_furniture" title="Louis XVI furniture">Louis XVI</a>), secretary (<a href="/wiki/Empire_style#Furniture" title="Empire style">Empire</a>), fauteuil a joues armchair (19th century <a href="/wiki/Eclecticism" title="Eclecticism">Eclecticism</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Revivalism_(architecture)" title="Revivalism (architecture)">Revivalism</a>), vitrine (<a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau_furniture" title="Art Nouveau furniture">Art Nouveau</a>), commode (<a href="/wiki/Art_Deco#Furniture" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a>), <a href="/wiki/IKEA" title="IKEA">IKEA</a> kitchen cupboards and a table with glass top (Contemporary)</div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Furniture</b> refers to movable objects intended to support various human activities such as seating (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Stool_(seat)" title="Stool (seat)">stools</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chair" title="Chair">chairs</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sofa" title="Sofa">sofas</a>), eating (<a href="/wiki/Table_(furniture)" title="Table (furniture)">tables</a>), storing items, eating and/or working with an item, and sleeping (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Bed" title="Bed">beds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hammock" title="Hammock">hammocks</a>). Furniture is also used to hold objects at a convenient height for work (as horizontal surfaces above the ground, such as tables and <a href="/wiki/Desk" title="Desk">desks</a>), or to store things (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cupboard" title="Cupboard">cupboards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shelf_(storage)" title="Shelf (storage)">shelves</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Drawer" title="Drawer">drawers</a>). Furniture can be a product of design and can be considered a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decorative_art" title="Decorative art">decorative art</a>. In addition to furniture's functional role, it can serve a <a href="/wiki/Symbol" title="Symbol">symbolic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious</a> purpose. It can be made from a vast multitude of materials, including <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>. Furniture can be made using a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woodworking_joints" title="Woodworking joints">woodworking joints</a> which often reflects the local culture.
</p><p>People have been using natural objects, such as tree stumps, rocks and moss, as furniture since the beginning of human civilization and continues today in some households/campsites. <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">Archaeological</a> research shows that from around 30,000 years ago, people started to construct and carve their own furniture, using wood, stone, and animal bones. Early furniture from this period is known from artwork such as a <a href="/wiki/Venus_figurine" title="Venus figurine">Venus figurine</a> found in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, depicting the goddess on a throne. The first surviving extant furniture is in the homes of <a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae" title="Skara Brae">Skara Brae</a> in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, and includes cupboards, dressers and beds all constructed from stone. Complex construction techniques such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joinery_(woodworking)" title="Joinery (woodworking)">joinery</a> began in the <a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Egypt)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Egypt)">early dynastic</a> period of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Era" title="Era">era</a> saw constructed wooden pieces, including <a href="/wiki/Stool_(seat)" title="Stool (seat)">stools</a> and tables, sometimes decorated with valuable metals or ivory. The evolution of furniture design continued in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Rome</a>, with thrones being commonplace as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Klinai" title="Klinai">klinai</a>, multipurpose couches used for relaxing, eating, and sleeping. The furniture of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> was usually heavy, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>, and ornamented. Furniture design expanded during the Italian <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> of the fourteenth and fifteenth century. The seventeenth century, in both Southern and Northern Europe, was characterized by opulent, often gilded <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> designs. The nineteenth century is usually defined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revival_styles" title="Revival styles">revival styles</a>. The first three-quarters of the twentieth century are often seen as the march towards <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">Modernism</a>. One unique outgrowth of post-modern furniture design is a return to natural shapes and textures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-povo_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-povo-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The English word <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/furniture" title="wikt:furniture">furniture</a></i> is derived from the French word <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fourniture" title="wikt:fourniture">fourniture</a></i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup> the noun form of <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fournir" title="wikt:fournir">fournir</a></i></span>, which means to supply or provide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Thus <i>fourniture</i> in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> means supplies or provisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The English usage, referring specifically to <a href="/wiki/House" title="House">household</a> objects, is specific to that language;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeekley2013609–610_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeekley2013609–610-5">[5]</a></sup> French and other <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance</a> languages as well as <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> use variants of the word <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/meubles" title="wikt:meubles">meubles</a></i>, which derives from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mobilia" title="wikt:mobilia">mobilia</a></i>, meaning "moveable goods".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolodow2010146_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolodow2010146-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The practice of using natural objects as rudimentary pieces of furniture likely dates to the beginning of human civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20154_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20154-7">[7]</a></sup> Early humans are likely to have used tree stumps as seats, rocks as rudimentary tables, and mossy areas for sleeping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20154_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20154-7">[7]</a></sup> During the late <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaeolithic" title="Palaeolithic">Paleolithic</a> or early <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period, from around 30,000 years ago, people began constructing and carving their own furniture, using wood, stone and animal bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20151_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20151-8">[8]</a></sup> The earliest evidence for the existence of constructed furniture is a <a href="/wiki/Venus_figurine" title="Venus figurine">Venus figurine</a> found at the <a href="/wiki/Gagarino" title="Gagarino">Gagarino</a> site in Russia, which depicts the goddess in a sitting position, on a throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20152_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20152-9">[9]</a></sup> A similar <a href="/wiki/Seated_Woman_of_%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="Seated Woman of Çatalhöyük">statue of a seated woman</a> was found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catal_Huyuk" title="Catal Huyuk">Catal Huyuk</a> in Turkey, dating to between 6000 and 5500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20154_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20154-7">[7]</a></sup> The inclusion of such a seat in the figurines implies that these were already common artefacts of that age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20152_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmardzewski20152-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>A range of unique stone furniture has been excavated in <a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae" title="Skara Brae">Skara Brae</a>, a Neolithic village in <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, Scotland The site dates from 3100 to 2500 BCE and due to a shortage of wood in Orkney, the people of Skara Brae were forced to build with stone, a readily available material that could be worked easily and turned into items for use within the household. Each house shows a high degree of sophistication and was equipped with an extensive assortment of stone furniture, ranging from cupboards, dressers, and beds to shelves, stone seats, and <a href="/wiki/Limpet" title="Limpet">limpet</a> tanks. The stone dresser was regarded as the most important as it symbolically faces the entrance in each house and is therefore the first item seen when entering, perhaps displaying symbolic objects, including decorative artwork such as several Neolithic <a href="/wiki/Carved_stone_balls" title="Carved stone balls">carved stone balls</a> also found at the site.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_furniture" title="Ancient furniture">Ancient furniture</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Furniture" title="Ancient Furniture">Ancient furniture</a> has been excavated from the 8th-century BCE <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygian</a> <a href="/wiki/Tumulus" title="Tumulus">tumulus</a>, the Midas Mound, in <a href="/wiki/Gordion_Furniture_and_Wooden_Artifacts" title="Gordion Furniture and Wooden Artifacts">Gordion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>. Pieces found here include <a href="/wiki/Table_(furniture)" title="Table (furniture)">tables</a> and inlaid serving stands. There are also surviving works from the 9th-8th-century BCE <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> palace of <a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">Nimrud</a>. The earliest surviving carpet, the <a href="/wiki/Pazyryk_culture" title="Pazyryk culture">Pazyryk</a> Carpet was discovered in a frozen tomb in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and has been dated between the 6th and 3rd century BCE.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Egypt">Ancient Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ancient Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Art_of_ancient_Egypt#Furniture" title="Art of ancient Egypt">Art of ancient Egypt § Furniture</a></div>
<p>Civilization in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> began with the clearance and irrigation of land along the banks of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Nile" title="River Nile">River Nile</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoebuck196651_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoebuck196651-10">[10]</a></sup> which began in about 6000 BCE. By that time, society in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a> was already engaged in organized agriculture and the construction of large buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Redford_6_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Redford_6-11">[11]</a></sup>  At this period, Egyptians in the southwestern corner of Egypt were herding cattle and also constructing large buildings. <a href="/wiki/Mortar_(masonry)" title="Mortar (masonry)">Mortar</a> was in use by around 4000 BCE The inhabitants of the Nile Valley and delta were self-sufficient and were raising barley and <a href="/wiki/Emmer" title="Emmer">emmer</a> (an early variety of wheat) and stored it in pits lined with reed mats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoebuck196652_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoebuck196652-12">[12]</a></sup> They raised cattle, goats and pigs and they wove linens and baskets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoebuck196652_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoebuck196652-12">[12]</a></sup> Evidence of furniture from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" title="Predynastic Egypt">predynastic period</a> is scarce, but samples from <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="First Dynasty of Egypt">First Dynasty</a> tombs indicate an already advanced use of furnishings in the houses of the age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMetropolitan_Museum_of_Art1999117_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMetropolitan_Museum_of_Art1999117-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Egypt)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Egypt)">dynastic period</a>, which began in around 3200 BCE, Egyptian art developed significantly, and this included furniture design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore20061_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore20061-14">[14]</a></sup> Egyptian furniture was primarily constructed using <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>, but other materials were sometimes used, such as <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200614_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200614-15">[15]</a></sup> and pieces were often adorned with gold, silver, ivory and ebony, for decoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200614_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200614-15">[15]</a></sup> Wood found in Egypt was not suitable for furniture construction, so it had to be imported into the country from other places,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore20061_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore20061-14">[14]</a></sup> particularly <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGadalla2007243_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGadalla2007243-16">[16]</a></sup> The scarcity of wood necessitated innovation in construction techniques. The use of <a href="/wiki/Scarf_joint" title="Scarf joint">scarf joints</a> to join two shorter pieces together and form a longer beam was one example of this,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmardzewski201513–14_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmardzewski201513–14-17">[17]</a></sup> as well as construction of <a href="/wiki/Wood_veneer" title="Wood veneer">veneers</a> in which low quality cheap wood was used as the main building material, with a thin layer of expensive wood on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmardzewski201514_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmardzewski201514-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest used seating furniture in the dynastic period was the <a href="/wiki/Stool_(seat)" title="Stool (seat)">stool</a>, which was used throughout Egyptian society, from the royal family down to ordinary citizens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200615_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200615-19">[19]</a></sup> Various different designs were used, including stools with four vertical legs, and others with crossed splayed legs; almost all had rectangular seats, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200615_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200615-19">[19]</a></sup> Examples include the workman's stool, a simple three legged structure with a concave seat, designed for comfort during labour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELitchfield20116_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELitchfield20116-20">[20]</a></sup> and the much more ornate folding stool, with crossed folding legs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELitchfield20116–7_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELitchfield20116–7-21">[21]</a></sup> which were decorated with carved duck heads and ivory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELitchfield20116–7_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELitchfield20116–7-21">[21]</a></sup> and had hinges made of <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200615_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200615-19">[19]</a></sup> Full <a href="/wiki/Chair" title="Chair">chairs</a> were much rarer in early Egypt, being limited to only wealthy and high ranking people, and seen as a status symbol; they did not reach ordinary households until the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Eighteenth Dynasty of Egypt">18th dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200617_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200617-22">[22]</a></sup> Early examples were formed by adding a straight back to a stool, while later chairs had an inclined back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200617_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200617-22">[22]</a></sup> Other furniture types in ancient Egypt include tables, which are heavily represented in art, but almost nonexistent as preserved items – perhaps because they were placed outside tombs rather than within,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200621_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200621-23">[23]</a></sup> as well as beds and storage chests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200622_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200622-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200624_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200624-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:20px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hatnefer%27s_Chair_MET_21M_CAT047R4_(cropped).jpg" title="Chair of Hatnefer; 1492–1473 BC; boxwood, cypress, ebony &amp; linen cord; height: 53 cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art"><img alt="Chair of Hatnefer; 1492–1473 BC; boxwood, cypress, ebony &amp; linen cord; height: 53 cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art" data-file-height="1990" data-file-width="2139" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Hatnefer%27s_Chair_MET_21M_CAT047R4_%28cropped%29.jpg/150px-Hatnefer%27s_Chair_MET_21M_CAT047R4_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Hatnefer%27s_Chair_MET_21M_CAT047R4_%28cropped%29.jpg/225px-Hatnefer%27s_Chair_MET_21M_CAT047R4_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Hatnefer%27s_Chair_MET_21M_CAT047R4_%28cropped%29.jpg/300px-Hatnefer%27s_Chair_MET_21M_CAT047R4_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">

</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Greece">Ancient Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ancient Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_illustrations_of_ancient_Greek_chairs,_each_being_notated_with_a_letter._a,_b-klismos,_and_c-chair.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1380" data-file-width="3396" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Three_illustrations_of_ancient_Greek_chairs%2C_each_being_notated_with_a_letter._a%2C_b-klismos%2C_and_c-chair.jpg/275px-Three_illustrations_of_ancient_Greek_chairs%2C_each_being_notated_with_a_letter._a%2C_b-klismos%2C_and_c-chair.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Three_illustrations_of_ancient_Greek_chairs%2C_each_being_notated_with_a_letter._a%2C_b-klismos%2C_and_c-chair.jpg/413px-Three_illustrations_of_ancient_Greek_chairs%2C_each_being_notated_with_a_letter._a%2C_b-klismos%2C_and_c-chair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Three_illustrations_of_ancient_Greek_chairs%2C_each_being_notated_with_a_letter._a%2C_b-klismos%2C_and_c-chair.jpg/550px-Three_illustrations_of_ancient_Greek_chairs%2C_each_being_notated_with_a_letter._a%2C_b-klismos%2C_and_c-chair.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_illustrations_of_ancient_Greek_chairs,_each_being_notated_with_a_letter._a,_b-klismos,_and_c-chair.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Three illustrations of ancient Greek chairs, each being notated with a letter: a, b-klismos, and c-chair</div></div></div>
<p>Historical knowledge of Greek furniture is derived from various sources, including <a href="/wiki/Greek_literature" title="Greek literature">literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a>, sculptures, statuettes, and painted vases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639-26">[26]</a></sup> Some pieces survive to this day, primarily those constructed from metals, including <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, or marble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639-26">[26]</a></sup> Wood was an important and common material in Greek furniture, both domestic and imported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639-26">[26]</a></sup> A common technique was to construct the main sections of the furniture with cheap solid wood, then apply a veneer using an expensive wood, such as maple or ebony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639-26">[26]</a></sup> Greek furniture construction also made use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dowels" title="Dowels">dowels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mortise_and_tenon" title="Mortise and tenon">tenons</a> for joining the wooden parts of a piece together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200639-26">[26]</a></sup> Wood was shaped by carving, steam treatment, and the lathe, and furniture is known to have been decorated with ivory, tortoise shell, glass, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> or other precious materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter1966125_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter1966125-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern word “<a href="/wiki/Throne" title="Throne">throne</a>” is derived from the ancient Greek <i>thronos</i> (Greek singular: θρόνος), which was a seat designated for deities or individuals of high status/hierarchy or honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter196613_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter196613-28">[28]</a></sup> The colossal chryselephantine <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Zeus_at_Olympia" title="Statue of Zeus at Olympia">statue of Zeus at Olympia</a>, constructed by <a href="/wiki/Phidias" title="Phidias">Phidias</a> and lost in antiquity, featured the god Zeus seated on an elaborate throne, which was decorated with gold, precious stones, ebony and ivory, according to <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter196614,_NH_5.11.2ff_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter196614,_NH_5.11.2ff-29">[29]</a></sup> Other Greek seats included the <i><a href="/wiki/Klismos" title="Klismos">klismos</a></i>, an elegant Greek chair with a curved backrest and legs whose form was copied by the Romans and is now part of the vocabulary of furniture design,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> the backless stool (<a href="/wiki/Diphros" title="Diphros">diphros</a>), which existed in most Greek <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homes" title="Homes">homes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and folding stool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWanscher198083_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWanscher198083-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Klinai" title="Klinai">kline</a>, used from the late seventh century BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> was a multipurpose piece used as a <a href="/wiki/Bed" title="Bed">bed</a>, but also as a <a href="/wiki/Couch" title="Couch">sofa</a> and for reclining during meals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200643_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200643-34">[34]</a></sup> It was rectangular and supported on four legs, two of which could be longer than the other, providing support for an armrest or headboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Mattresses, rugs, and blankets may have been used, but there is no evidence for sheets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200643_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200643-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, Greek tables were low and often appear in depictions alongside <i>klinai</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter196663_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter196663-36">[36]</a></sup> The most common type of Greek table had a rectangular top supported on three legs, although numerous configurations exist, including trapezoid and circular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200642_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200642-37">[37]</a></sup> Tables in ancient Greece were used mostly for dining purposes – in depictions of banquets, it appears as though each participant would have utilized a single table, rather than a collective use of a larger piece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichter196666_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichter196666-38">[38]</a></sup> Tables also figured prominently in religious contexts, as indicated in vase paintings, for example, the wine vessel associated with Dionysus, dating to around 450 BCE and now housed at the <a href="/wiki/Art_Institute_of_Chicago" title="Art Institute of Chicago">Art Institute of Chicago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chest" title="Chest">Chests</a> were used for storage of clothes and personal items and were usually rectangular with hinged lids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200642_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200642-37">[37]</a></sup> Chests depicted in terracotta show elaborate patterns and design, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_fret" title="Greek fret">Greek fret</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200643_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200643-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Rome">Ancient Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ancient Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Roman furniture was based heavily on Greek furniture, in style and construction. Rome gradually superseded Greece as the foremost culture of Europe, leading eventually to Greece becoming a province of Rome in 146 BC. Rome thus took over production and distribution of Greek furniture, and the boundary between the two is blurred. The Romans did have some limited innovation outside of Greek influence, and styles distinctly their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200661_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200661-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>Roman furniture was constructed principally using wood, metal and stone, with marble and limestone used for outside furniture. Very little wooden furniture survives intact, but there is evidence that a variety of woods were used, including maple, citron, beech, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>, and holly. Some imported wood such as satinwood was used for decoration. The most commonly used metal was bronze, of which numerous examples have survived, for example, headrests for couches and metal stools. Similar to the Greeks, <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Demography of the Roman Empire">Romans</a> used tenons, dowels, nails, and glue to join wooden pieces together, and also practised veneering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200661_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlakemore200661-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1738 and 1748 excavations of <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">Herculaneum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> revealed Roman furniture, preserved in the ashes of the AD 79 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eruption_of_Mount_Vesuvius_in_79" title="Eruption of Mount Vesuvius in 79">eruption of Vesuvius</a>.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Handbook_of_ornament;_a_grammar_of_art,_industrial_and_architectural_designing_in_all_its_branches,_for_practical_as_well_as_theoretical_use_(1900)_(14804265373).jpg" title="Illustration of Roman furniture details, from 1900, very similar with Empire style furniture"><img alt="Illustration of Roman furniture details, from 1900, very similar with Empire style furniture" data-file-height="2438" data-file-width="1520" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Handbook_of_ornament%3B_a_grammar_of_art%2C_industrial_and_architectural_designing_in_all_its_branches%2C_for_practical_as_well_as_theoretical_use_%281900%29_%2814804265373%29.jpg/93px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Handbook_of_ornament%3B_a_grammar_of_art%2C_industrial_and_architectural_designing_in_all_its_branches%2C_for_practical_as_well_as_theoretical_use_%281900%29_%2814804265373%29.jpg/140px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Handbook_of_ornament%3B_a_grammar_of_art%2C_industrial_and_architectural_designing_in_all_its_branches%2C_for_practical_as_well_as_theoretical_use_%281900%29_%2814804265373%29.jpg/187px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="93"/></a></div></div>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clevelandart_1995.10.jpg" title="Tripod base; circa 100 BC; bronze; overall: 77 x 32.3 x 28 cm; Cleveland Museum of Art (Cleveland, Ohio, USA)"><img alt="Tripod base; circa 100 BC; bronze; overall: 77 x 32.3 x 28 cm; Cleveland Museum of Art (Cleveland, Ohio, USA)" data-file-height="3400" data-file-width="3260" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Clevelandart_1995.10.jpg/144px-Clevelandart_1995.10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Clevelandart_1995.10.jpg/216px-Clevelandart_1995.10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Clevelandart_1995.10.jpg/288px-Clevelandart_1995.10.jpg 2x" width="144"/></a></div></div>

</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:24px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forziere_con_sacrificio_a_giove,_I_secolo_dc,_legno,_ferro_e_bronzo,_con_ageminature,_dalla_casa_di_trittolemo_a_pompei_(napoli,_man)_01.jpg" title="Treasure chest with a sacrifice of Jupiter depicted on it; 1st century AD; wood, iron and bronze, with ageminature; from Pompeii; Naples National Archaeological Museum (Naples, Italy)"><img alt="Treasure chest with a sacrifice of Jupiter depicted on it; 1st century AD; wood, iron and bronze, with ageminature; from Pompeii; Naples National Archaeological Museum (Naples, Italy)" data-file-height="1736" data-file-width="1968" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Forziere_con_sacrificio_a_giove%2C_I_secolo_dc%2C_legno%2C_ferro_e_bronzo%2C_con_ageminature%2C_dalla_casa_di_trittolemo_a_pompei_%28napoli%2C_man%29_01.jpg/150px-Forziere_con_sacrificio_a_giove%2C_I_secolo_dc%2C_legno%2C_ferro_e_bronzo%2C_con_ageminature%2C_dalla_casa_di_trittolemo_a_pompei_%28napoli%2C_man%29_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Forziere_con_sacrificio_a_giove%2C_I_secolo_dc%2C_legno%2C_ferro_e_bronzo%2C_con_ageminature%2C_dalla_casa_di_trittolemo_a_pompei_%28napoli%2C_man%29_01.jpg/225px-Forziere_con_sacrificio_a_giove%2C_I_secolo_dc%2C_legno%2C_ferro_e_bronzo%2C_con_ageminature%2C_dalla_casa_di_trittolemo_a_pompei_%28napoli%2C_man%29_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Forziere_con_sacrificio_a_giove%2C_I_secolo_dc%2C_legno%2C_ferro_e_bronzo%2C_con_ageminature%2C_dalla_casa_di_trittolemo_a_pompei_%28napoli%2C_man%29_01.jpg/300px-Forziere_con_sacrificio_a_giove%2C_I_secolo_dc%2C_legno%2C_ferro_e_bronzo%2C_con_ageminature%2C_dalla_casa_di_trittolemo_a_pompei_%28napoli%2C_man%29_01.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div>

<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Credenza_MET_DP255160.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2266" data-file-width="3923" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Credenza_MET_DP255160.jpg/300px-Credenza_MET_DP255160.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Credenza_MET_DP255160.jpg/450px-Credenza_MET_DP255160.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Credenza_MET_DP255160.jpg/600px-Credenza_MET_DP255160.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Credenza_MET_DP255160.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic</a> credenza; 1440–1450; walnut and intarsia; 147.3 x 317.5 x 63.5 cm; <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a> (New York City)</div></div></div>
<p>In contrast to the ancient civilizations of Egypt, Greece, and Rome, there is comparatively little evidence of furniture from the 5th to the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197933_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197933-41">[41]</a></sup> Very few extant pieces survive, and evidence in literature is also scarce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197933_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197933-41">[41]</a></sup> It is likely that the style of furniture prevalent in late antiquity persisted throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_ages" title="Middle ages">middle ages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197933_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197933-41">[41]</a></sup> For example, a throne similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> is depicted in a sixth-century <a href="/wiki/Diptych" title="Diptych">diptych</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197933_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197933-41">[41]</a></sup> while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayeux_tapestry" title="Bayeux tapestry">Bayeux tapestry</a> shows <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Godwinson" title="Harold Godwinson">Harold</a> seated on seats similar to the Roman <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sella_curulis" title="Sella curulis">sella curulis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197935_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELucie-Smith197935-42">[42]</a></sup> The furniture of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> was usually heavy, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>, and ornamented with carved designs.
</p><p>The Hellenistic influence upon <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_art" title="Byzantine art">Byzantine</a> furniture can be seen through the use of <a href="/wiki/Acanthus_(ornament)" title="Acanthus (ornament)">acanthus leaves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palmette" title="Palmette">palmettes</a>, bay and olive leaves as ornaments. Oriental influences manifest through <a href="/wiki/Rosette_(design)" title="Rosette (design)">rosettes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabesque" title="Arabesque">arabesques</a> and the geometric stylisation of certain vegetal motifs. Christianity brings symbols in Byzantine ornamentation: the pigeon, fishes, the lamb and vines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991172_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991172-43">[43]</a></sup> The furniture from Byzantine houses and palaces was usually luxurious, highly decorated and finely ornamented. Stone, marble, metal, wood and ivory are used. Surfaces and ornaments are gilded, painted plychrome, plated with sheets of gold, emailed in bright colors, and covered in precious stones. The variety of Byzantine furniture is pretty big: tables with square, rectangle or round top, sumptuous decorated, made of wood sometimes inlaid, with bronze, ivory or <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> ornaments; chairs with high backs and with wool blankets or animal furs, with coloured pillows, and then banks and stools; wardrobes were used only for storing books; cloths and valuable objects were kept in chests, with iron locks; the form of beds imitated the Roman ones, but have different designs of legs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991174_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991174-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main ornament of <a href="/wiki/Gothic_art" title="Gothic art">Gothic</a> furniture and all applied arts is the <i><a href="/wiki/Ogive" title="Ogive">ogive</a></i>. The geometric <a href="/wiki/Rosette_(design)" title="Rosette (design)">rosette</a> accompanies the ogive many times, having a big variety of forms. Architectural elements are used at furniture, at the beginning with purely decorative reasons, but later as structure elements. Besides the ogive, the main ornaments are: acanthus leaves, ivy, oak leaves, haulms, clovers, <a href="/wiki/Fleur-de-lis" title="Fleur-de-lis">fleurs-de-lis</a>, knights with shields, heads with crowns and characters from the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>. Chests are the main type of Gothic furniture used by the majority of the population. Usually, the locks and escutcheon of chests have also an ornamental scope, being finely made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991206,_207,_209,_210_&amp;_211_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991206,_207,_209,_210_&amp;_211-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance">Renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_style" title="Henry II style">Henry II style</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_style" title="Henry IV style">Henry IV style</a></div>
<p>Along with the other arts, the Italian <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> of the fourteenth and fifteenth century marked a rebirth in design, often inspired by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Roman" title="Greco-Roman">Greco-Roman</a> tradition. A similar explosion of design, and renaissance of culture in general occurred in Northern Europe, starting in the fifteenth century.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%89couen_(95),_ch%C3%A2teau,_%C3%A9tage,_appt_du_conn%C3%A9table_8.jpg" title="Sideboard; c.1524; wood; height: 144 cm; Château d'Écouen (Écouen, France)[48]"><img alt="Sideboard; c.1524; wood; height: 144 cm; Château d'Écouen (Écouen, France)[48]" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/%C3%89couen_%2895%29%2C_ch%C3%A2teau%2C_%C3%A9tage%2C_appt_du_conn%C3%A9table_8.jpg/112px-%C3%89couen_%2895%29%2C_ch%C3%A2teau%2C_%C3%A9tage%2C_appt_du_conn%C3%A9table_8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/%C3%89couen_%2895%29%2C_ch%C3%A2teau%2C_%C3%A9tage%2C_appt_du_conn%C3%A9table_8.jpg/169px-%C3%89couen_%2895%29%2C_ch%C3%A2teau%2C_%C3%A9tage%2C_appt_du_conn%C3%A9table_8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/%C3%89couen_%2895%29%2C_ch%C3%A2teau%2C_%C3%A9tage%2C_appt_du_conn%C3%A9table_8.jpg/225px-%C3%89couen_%2895%29%2C_ch%C3%A2teau%2C_%C3%A9tage%2C_appt_du_conn%C3%A9table_8.jpg 2x" width="112"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Sideboard; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>1524; wood; height: 144 cm; <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teau_d%27%C3%89couen" title="Château d'Écouen">Château d'Écouen</a> (<a href="/wiki/%C3%89couen" title="Écouen">Écouen</a>, France)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="17th_and_18th_centuries">17th and 18th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 17th and 18th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Baroque#Furniture" title="Baroque">Baroque § Furniture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rococo#Furniture_and_decoration" title="Rococo">Rococo § Furniture and decoration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism#Architecture_and_the_decorative_arts" title="Neoclassicism">Neoclassicism § Architecture and the decorative arts</a></div>
<p>The 17th century, in both Southern and Northern Europe, was characterized by opulent, often gilded <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> designs that frequently incorporated a profusion of vegetal and scrolling ornament. Starting in the eighteenth century, furniture designs began to develop more rapidly. Although there were some styles that belonged primarily to one nation, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palladianism" title="Palladianism">Palladianism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Quinze" title="Louis Quinze">Louis Quinze</a> in <a href="/wiki/French_furniture" title="French furniture">French furniture</a>, others, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism" title="Neoclassicism">Neoclassicism</a> were perpetuated throughout Western Europe.
</p><p>During the 18th century, the fashion was set in England by the French art. In the beginning of the century <a href="/wiki/Boulle" title="Boulle">Boulle</a> cabinets were at the peak of their popularity and <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a> was reigning in France. In this era, most of the furniture had metal and enamelled decorations in it and some of the furniture was covered in inlays of marbles lapis lazuli, and porphyry and other stones. By mid-century this <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> style was displaced by the graceful curves, shining <a href="/wiki/Ormolu" title="Ormolu">ormolu</a>, and intricate <a href="/wiki/Marquetry" title="Marquetry">marquetry</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a> style, which in turn gave way around 1770 to the more severe lines of <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism" title="Neoclassicism">Neoclassicism</a>, modeled after the architecture of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_history_of_feminine_fashion_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_history_of_feminine_fashion-52">[52]</a></sup> Creating a mass market for furniture, the distinguished London cabinet maker <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Chippendale" title="Thomas Chippendale">Thomas Chippendale</a>'s <i>The Gentleman and Cabinet Maker's Director</i> (1754) is regarded as the "first comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Trade_literature" title="Trade literature">trade catalogue</a> of its kind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is something so distinct in the development of taste in French furniture, marked out by the three styles to which the three monarchs have given the name of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Style_Louis_XIV" title="Style Louis XIV">Louis Quatorze</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Quinze" title="Louis Quinze">Louis Quinze</a>", and "Louis Seize". This will be evident to anyone who will visit, first the <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Versailles" title="Palace of Versailles">Palace of Versailles</a>, then the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Trianon" title="Grand Trianon">Grand Trianon</a>, and afterwards the <a href="/wiki/Petit_Trianon" title="Petit Trianon">Petit Trianon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELitchfield2011211_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELitchfield2011211-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chevy_Chase_Sideboard.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2436" data-file-width="3532" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Chevy_Chase_Sideboard.png/220px-Chevy_Chase_Sideboard.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Chevy_Chase_Sideboard.png/330px-Chevy_Chase_Sideboard.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Chevy_Chase_Sideboard.png/440px-Chevy_Chase_Sideboard.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chevy_Chase_Sideboard.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Chevy Chase Sideboard by Gerrard Robinson. Often considered to be one of the finest furniture pieces of the 19th century and an icon of Victorian furniture.</div></div></div>
<p>The nineteenth century is usually defined by concurrent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revival_styles" title="Revival styles">revival styles</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothic</a>, Neoclassicism, and Rococo. The design <a href="/wiki/Reform" title="Reform">reforms</a> of the late century introduced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aesthetic_movement" title="Aesthetic movement">Aesthetic movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arts_and_Crafts_movement" title="Arts and Crafts movement">Arts and Crafts movement</a>. <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a> was influenced by both of these movements. <a href="/wiki/Shakers" title="Shakers">Shaker</a>-style furniture became popular during this time in North America as well.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Desk_chair_(fauteuil_de_bureau)_MET_DP278961.jpg" title="Empire desk chair; c.1805–1808; mahogany, gilt bronze and satin-velvet upholstery; 87.6 × 59.7 × 64.8 cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art (New York City)"><img alt="Empire desk chair; c.1805–1808; mahogany, gilt bronze and satin-velvet upholstery; 87.6 × 59.7 × 64.8 cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art (New York City)" data-file-height="1942" data-file-width="1502" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Desk_chair_%28fauteuil_de_bureau%29_MET_DP278961.jpg/116px-Desk_chair_%28fauteuil_de_bureau%29_MET_DP278961.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Desk_chair_%28fauteuil_de_bureau%29_MET_DP278961.jpg/174px-Desk_chair_%28fauteuil_de_bureau%29_MET_DP278961.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Desk_chair_%28fauteuil_de_bureau%29_MET_DP278961.jpg/232px-Desk_chair_%28fauteuil_de_bureau%29_MET_DP278961.jpg 2x" width="116"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Empire_style" title="Empire style">Empire</a> desk chair; <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr>1805–1808; mahogany, gilt bronze and satin-velvet upholstery; 87.6 × 59.7 × 64.8 cm; <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a> (New York City)
</p>
</div>
</div></li>

<div class="thumb" style="width: 180px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%89mile_gall%C3%A9,_bureau_%27foresta_lorenese%27,_1900.JPG" title="Desk (Art Nouveau), presented at the 1900 Paris Exposition; by Émile Gallé; 1900; molded and carved oak, with chiseled and patinated bronze; height: 108.5 cm; Musée d'Orsay (Paris)[68]"><img alt="Desk (Art Nouveau), presented at the 1900 Paris Exposition; by Émile Gallé; 1900; molded and carved oak, with chiseled and patinated bronze; height: 108.5 cm; Musée d'Orsay (Paris)[68]" data-file-height="2036" data-file-width="1608" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/%C3%89mile_gall%C3%A9%2C_bureau_%27foresta_lorenese%27%2C_1900.JPG/118px-%C3%89mile_gall%C3%A9%2C_bureau_%27foresta_lorenese%27%2C_1900.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/%C3%89mile_gall%C3%A9%2C_bureau_%27foresta_lorenese%27%2C_1900.JPG/178px-%C3%89mile_gall%C3%A9%2C_bureau_%27foresta_lorenese%27%2C_1900.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/%C3%89mile_gall%C3%A9%2C_bureau_%27foresta_lorenese%27%2C_1900.JPG/237px-%C3%89mile_gall%C3%A9%2C_bureau_%27foresta_lorenese%27%2C_1900.JPG 2x" width="118"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Desk (Art Nouveau), presented at the <a href="/wiki/Exposition_Universelle_(1900)" title="Exposition Universelle (1900)">1900 Paris Exposition</a>; by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Gall%C3%A9" title="Émile Gallé">Émile Gallé</a>; 1900; molded and carved oak, with chiseled and patinated bronze; height: 108.5 cm; <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_d%27Orsay" title="Musée d'Orsay">Musée d'Orsay</a> (Paris)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_North_American">Early North American</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Early North American">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This design was in many ways rooted in necessity and emphasizes both form and materials. Early British Colonial American<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (May 2015)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> chairs and tables are often constructed with turned spindles and chair backs often constructed with steaming to bend the wood. Wood choices tend to be deciduous hardwoods with a particular emphasis on the wood of edible or fruit bearing trees such as cherry or walnut.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modernism">Modernism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Modernism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau_furniture" title="Art Nouveau furniture">Art Nouveau furniture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rietveld_chair_1b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1158" data-file-width="819" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Rietveld_chair_1b.jpg/170px-Rietveld_chair_1b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Rietveld_chair_1b.jpg/255px-Rietveld_chair_1b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Rietveld_chair_1b.jpg/340px-Rietveld_chair_1b.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rietveld_chair_1b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Red_and_Blue_Chair" title="Red and Blue Chair">Red and Blue Chair</a> (1917), designed by <a href="/wiki/Gerrit_Rietveld" title="Gerrit Rietveld">Gerrit Rietveld</a></div></div></div>
<p>The first three-quarters of the twentieth century is often seen as the march towards <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">Modernism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a>, <a href="/wiki/De_Stijl" title="De Stijl">De Stijl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus" title="Bauhaus">Bauhaus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jugendstil" title="Jugendstil">Jugendstil</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wiener_Werkst%C3%A4tte_Style" title="Wiener Werkstätte Style">Wiener Werkstätte</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Secession" title="Vienna Secession">Vienna Secession</a> designers all worked to some degree within the Modernist idiom. Born from the <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus" title="Bauhaus">Bauhaus</a> and Art Deco/Streamline styles came the post WWII "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Century_Modern" title="Mid-Century Modern">Mid-Century Modern</a>" style using materials developed during the war including laminated <a href="/wiki/Plywood" title="Plywood">plywood</a>, plastics, and fiberglass. Prime examples include furniture designed by George Nelson Associates, Charles and Ray Eames, <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCobb" title="Paul McCobb">Paul McCobb</a>, Florence Knoll, Harry Bertoia, Eero Saarinen, Harvey Probber, Vladamir Kagan and <a href="/wiki/Danish_modern" title="Danish modern">Danish modern</a> designers including <a href="/wiki/Finn_Juhl" title="Finn Juhl">Finn Juhl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arne_Jacobsen" title="Arne Jacobsen">Arne Jacobsen</a>. <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">Postmodern</a> design, intersecting the <a href="/wiki/Pop_art" title="Pop art">Pop art</a> movement, gained steam in the 1960s and 70s, promoted in the 80s by groups such as the Italy-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memphis_movement" title="Memphis movement">Memphis movement</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transitional_furniture" title="Transitional furniture">Transitional furniture</a> is intended to fill a place between Traditional and Modern tastes.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stainless_Steel_Talbe_with_FSC_Eucalyptus_wood_Ecodesign.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1568" data-file-width="2352" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Stainless_Steel_Talbe_with_FSC_Eucalyptus_wood_Ecodesign.JPG/220px-Stainless_Steel_Talbe_with_FSC_Eucalyptus_wood_Ecodesign.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Stainless_Steel_Talbe_with_FSC_Eucalyptus_wood_Ecodesign.JPG/330px-Stainless_Steel_Talbe_with_FSC_Eucalyptus_wood_Ecodesign.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Stainless_Steel_Talbe_with_FSC_Eucalyptus_wood_Ecodesign.JPG/440px-Stainless_Steel_Talbe_with_FSC_Eucalyptus_wood_Ecodesign.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stainless_Steel_Talbe_with_FSC_Eucalyptus_wood_Ecodesign.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stainless Steel Table with <a href="/wiki/Forest_Stewardship_Council" title="Forest Stewardship Council">FSC</a> Teca Wood - <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> Ecodesign</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecodesign">Ecodesign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ecodesign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Great efforts from individuals, governments, and companies has led to the manufacturing of products with higher sustainability known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecodesign" title="Ecodesign">Ecodesign</a>. This new line of furniture is based on environmentally friendly design. Its use and popularity are increasing each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary">Contemporary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Contemporary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One unique outgrowth of post-modern furniture design is <a href="/wiki/Live_edge" title="Live edge">Live edge</a>, heralding a return to natural shapes and textures within the home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-povo_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-povo-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_history">Asian history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Asian history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asian_furniture" title="Asian furniture">Asian furniture</a> has a quite distinct history. The traditions out of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, Indonesia (Bali and Java) and <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> are some of the best known, but places such as <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, and the countries of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_East_Asia" title="South East Asia">South East Asia</a> have unique facets of their own.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Far_Eastern">Far Eastern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Far Eastern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:327px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moon-gate_bed_shown_in_the_Philadelphia_Centennial_Exposition,_Ningbo,_China,_detail,_c._1876,_satinwood_(huang_lu),_other_Asian_woods,_ivory_-_Peabody_Essex_Museum_-_DSC07355.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Moon-gate_bed_shown_in_the_Philadelphia_Centennial_Exposition%2C_Ningbo%2C_China%2C_detail%2C_c._1876%2C_satinwood_%28huang_lu%29%2C_other_Asian_woods%2C_ivory_-_Peabody_Essex_Museum_-_DSC07355.jpg/325px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Moon-gate_bed_shown_in_the_Philadelphia_Centennial_Exposition%2C_Ningbo%2C_China%2C_detail%2C_c._1876%2C_satinwood_%28huang_lu%29%2C_other_Asian_woods%2C_ivory_-_Peabody_Essex_Museum_-_DSC07355.jpg/488px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Moon-gate_bed_shown_in_the_Philadelphia_Centennial_Exposition%2C_Ningbo%2C_China%2C_detail%2C_c._1876%2C_satinwood_%28huang_lu%29%2C_other_Asian_woods%2C_ivory_-_Peabody_Essex_Museum_-_DSC07355.jpg/650px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="325"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moon-gate_bed_shown_in_the_Philadelphia_Centennial_Exposition,_Ningbo,_China,_detail,_c._1876,_satinwood_(huang_lu),_other_Asian_woods,_ivory_-_Peabody_Essex_Museum_-_DSC07355.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of a Chinese moon-gate bed from circa 1876</div></div></div>
<p>The use of uncarved wood and bamboo and the use of heavy lacquers are well known Chinese styles. It is worth noting that Chinese furniture varies dramatically from one dynasty to the next. Chinese ornamentation is highly inspired by paintings, with floral and plant life motifs including bamboo trees, chrysanthemums, waterlilies, irises, magnolias, flowers and branches of cherry, apple, apricot and plum, or elongated bamboo leaves; animal ornaments include lions, bulls, ducks, peacocks, parrots, pheasants, roosters, <a href="/wiki/Ibis" title="Ibis">ibises</a> and butterflies. The dragon is the symbol of earth fertility, and of the power and wisdom of the emperor. Lacquers are mostly populated with princesses, various Chinese people, soldiers, children, ritually and daily scenes. Architectural features tend toward geometric ornaments, like <a href="/wiki/Meander_(art)" title="Meander (art)">meanders</a> and labyrinths. The interior of a Chinese house was simple and sober. All Chinese furniture is made of wood, usually ebony, <a href="/wiki/Teak" title="Teak">teak</a>, or rosewood for heavier furniture (chairs, tables and benches) and bamboo, pine and larch for lighter furniture (stools and small chairs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991152,_153,_154_&amp;_156_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991152,_153,_154_&amp;_156-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional Japanese furniture is well known for its <a href="/wiki/Minimalism" title="Minimalism">minimalist</a> style, extensive use of wood, high-quality craftsmanship and reliance on wood grain instead of painting or thick lacquer. Japanese chests are known as <a href="/wiki/Tansu" title="Tansu">Tansu</a>, known for elaborate decorative iron work, and are some of the most sought-after of Japanese antiques. The antiques available generally date back to the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Tokugawa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meiji_period" title="Meiji period">Meiji periods</a>. Both the technique of lacquering and the specific lacquer (resin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhus_vernicifera" title="Rhus vernicifera">Rhus vernicifera</a>) originated in China, but the lacquer tree also grows well in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>. The recipes of preparation are original to Japan: resin is mixed with wheat flour, clay or pottery powder, <a href="/wiki/Turpentine" title="Turpentine">turpentine</a>, iron powder or wood coal. In ornamentation, the chrysanthemum, known as kiku, the national flower, is a very popular ornament, including the 16-petal chrysanthemum symbolizing the Emperor. Cherry and apple flowers are used for decorating screens, vases and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dji" title="Shōji">shōji</a>. Common animal ornaments include dragons, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_carp" title="Common carp">carps</a>, cranes, gooses, tigers, horses and monkeys; representations of architecture such as houses, pavilions, towers, <a href="/wiki/Torii" title="Torii">torii</a> gates, bridges and temples are also common. The furniture of a Japanese house consists of tables, shelves, wardrobes, small holders for flowers, <a href="/wiki/Bonsai_styles" title="Bonsai styles">bonsais</a> or for <a href="/wiki/Bonkei" title="Bonkei">bonkei</a>, boxes, lanterns with wooden frames and translucent paper, neck and elbow holders, and <a href="/wiki/Jardiniere" title="Jardiniere">jardinieres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991164,_165_&amp;_166_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBucătaru1991164,_165_&amp;_166-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 185px"><div style="width: 185px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_furniture_types" title="List of furniture types">List of furniture types</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="For_sitting">For sitting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: For sitting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Seating is amongst the oldest known furniture types, and authors including <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a> regard it as the most important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup> In addition to the functional design, seating has had an important decorative element from ancient times to the present day. This includes carved and sculpted pieces intended as works of art, as well as the styling of seats to indicate social importance, with senior figures or leaders granted the use of specially designed seats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopædia_Britannica_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopædia_Britannica-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The simplest form of seat is the <a href="/wiki/Chair" title="Chair">chair</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> which is a piece of furniture designed to allow a single person to sit down, which has a back and legs, as well as a platform for sitting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Chairs often feature cushions made from various fabrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Petit_fauteuil_de_Tout%C3%A2nkhamon_2019.jpg" title="Ancient Egyptian armchair of Tutankhamun; 1336-1326 BC; wood, ebony, ivory and gold leaf; height: 71 cm; Exposition of Tutankhamun Treasure in Paris (2019)"><img alt="Ancient Egyptian armchair of Tutankhamun; 1336-1326 BC; wood, ebony, ivory and gold leaf; height: 71 cm; Exposition of Tutankhamun Treasure in Paris (2019)" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Petit_fauteuil_de_Tout%C3%A2nkhamon_2019.jpg/127px-Petit_fauteuil_de_Tout%C3%A2nkhamon_2019.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Petit_fauteuil_de_Tout%C3%A2nkhamon_2019.jpg/191px-Petit_fauteuil_de_Tout%C3%A2nkhamon_2019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Petit_fauteuil_de_Tout%C3%A2nkhamon_2019.jpg/255px-Petit_fauteuil_de_Tout%C3%A2nkhamon_2019.jpg 2x" width="127"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Set_of_fourteen_side_chairs_MET_DP110780.jpg" title="Neoclassical chair; circa 1772; mahogany, covered in modern red Morocco leather; height: 97.2 cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art (New York City)"><img alt="Neoclassical chair; circa 1772; mahogany, covered in modern red Morocco leather; height: 97.2 cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art (New York City)" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3867" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Set_of_fourteen_side_chairs_MET_DP110780.jpg/164px-Set_of_fourteen_side_chairs_MET_DP110780.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Set_of_fourteen_side_chairs_MET_DP110780.jpg/247px-Set_of_fourteen_side_chairs_MET_DP110780.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Set_of_fourteen_side_chairs_MET_DP110780.jpg/329px-Set_of_fourteen_side_chairs_MET_DP110780.jpg 2x" width="164"/></a></div></div>

codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="707032" data-framerate="25" data-height="360" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d5/Video_of_making_of_log_furniture_cutting_a_bar_stool_from_a_log.webm/Video_of_making_of_log_furniture_cutting_a_bar_stool_from_a_log.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Video_of_making_of_log_furniture_cutting_a_bar_stool_from_a_log.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Making of <a href="/wiki/Log_furniture" title="Log furniture">log furniture</a>: cutting a bar stool from a piece of log</div></div></div>
<p>All different types of woods have unique signature marks that can help in easy identification of the type. Both <a href="/wiki/Hardwood" title="Hardwood">hardwoods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Softwood" title="Softwood">softwoods</a> are used in furniture manufacturing, and each has its own specific uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
Common softwoods used include pine, redwood and yew. Higher quality furniture tends to be made out of <a href="/wiki/Hardwood" title="Hardwood">hardwood</a>, including oak, maple, mahogany, teak, walnut, cherry and birch. Highest quality wood will have been air dried to rid it of its moisture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Standards_for_design.2C_functionality_and_safety"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Standards_for_design,_functionality_and_safety">Standards for design, functionality and safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Furniture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Standards for design, functionality and safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
